	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Aramaic</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">&#160;&#160;(Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=Aramaic_language&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" title="Aramaic language">Aramaic language</a>)</span></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Semitic language spread by the Neo-Assyrians</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Aramaic (disambiguation)">Aramaic (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the Semitic language now spoken by smaller numbers of people in scattered locations. For the Semitic language spoken in Ethiopia, see <a href="/wiki/Amharic" title="Amharic">Amharic</a>.</div>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;width:22em;"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold;background-color:  #faecc8;">Aramaic</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color:  #faecc8;"><span lang="arc" dir="rtl" title="Aramaic language text">ܐܪܡܝܐ</span>&#8206;, <span lang="he" dir="rtl" title="Hebrew language text">ארמיא</span><br /><i>Arāmāyā</i></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Geographic<br />distribution</th><td><a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fertile_Crescent" title="Fertile Crescent">Fertile Crescent</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Arabia" title="Eastern Arabia">Eastern Arabia</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Language_family" title="Language family">Linguistic classification</a></th><td><a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_languages" title="Afroasiatic languages">Afro-Asiatic</a><ul style="line-height:100%; margin-left:15px;padding-left:0"><li><a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic</a><ul style="line-height:100%; margin-left:5px;padding-left:0"><li><a href="/wiki/West_Semitic_languages" title="West Semitic languages">West Semitic</a><ul style="line-height:100%; margin-left:5px;padding-left:0"><li><a href="/wiki/Central_Semitic_languages" title="Central Semitic languages">Central Semitic</a><ul style="line-height:100%; margin-left:5px;padding-left:0"><li><a href="/wiki/Northwest_Semitic_languages" title="Northwest Semitic languages">Northwest Semitic</a><ul style="line-height:100%; margin-left:5px;padding-left:0"><li><b>Aramaic</b></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;">Early form</div></th><td><div style="text-align:left;"><div class="plainlist">

</div>
</div></td></tr><tr class="plainlist"><th scope="row">Subdivisions</th><td>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Eastern_Aramaic_languages" title="Eastern Aramaic languages">Eastern Aramaic</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Western_Aramaic_languages" title="Western Aramaic languages">Western Aramaic</a></li></ul>
</td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/ISO_639-2" title="ISO 639-2">ISO 639-2</a> / <a href="/wiki/ISO_639-5" title="ISO 639-5">5</a></th><td><samp><a href="https://iso639-3.sil.org/code/arc" class="extiw" title="iso639-3:arc">arc</a></samp></td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Linguasphere_Observatory" title="Linguasphere Observatory">Linguasphere</a></th><td><samp>12-AAA</samp></td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Glottolog" title="Glottolog">Glottolog</a></th><td><samp><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://glottolog.org/resource/languoid/id/aram1259">aram1259</a></samp><sup id="cite_ref-Glottolog_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glottolog-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Syriac_Aramaic.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Syriac_Aramaic.svg/220px-Syriac_Aramaic.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="62" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Syriac_Aramaic.svg/330px-Syriac_Aramaic.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Syriac_Aramaic.svg/440px-Syriac_Aramaic.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="280" data-file-height="79" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Syriac_Aramaic.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Arāmāyā</i> in Syriac Esṭrangelā script</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aramaic_alphabet.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Aramaic_alphabet.svg/220px-Aramaic_alphabet.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="55" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Aramaic_alphabet.svg/330px-Aramaic_alphabet.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Aramaic_alphabet.svg/440px-Aramaic_alphabet.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="375" data-file-height="93" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aramaic_alphabet.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Aramaic_alphabet" title="Aramaic alphabet">Syriac-Aramaic alphabet</a></div></div></div>
<p><b>Aramaic</b><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> (<i>Arāmāyā</i>; <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_alphabet" title="Hebrew alphabet">square script</a> <span lang="tmr" dir="rtl" title="Jewish Babylonian Aramaic (ca. 200-1200 CE) language text">אַרָמָיָא</span>, <a href="/wiki/Classical_Syriac_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Classical Syriac language">Classical Syriac</a>: <span lang="syc" dir="rtl">ܐܪܡܝܐ</span>&#8206;) is a language or group of languages belonging to the <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic subfamily</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_languages" title="Afroasiatic languages">Afroasiatic language family</a>. More specifically, it is part of the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Semitic_languages" title="Northwest Semitic languages">Northwest Semitic group</a>, which also includes the <a href="/wiki/Canaanite_languages" title="Canaanite languages">Canaanite languages</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phoenician_language" title="Phoenician language">Phoenician</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_alphabet" title="Aramaic alphabet">Aramaic alphabet</a> was widely adopted for other languages and is ancestral to the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_alphabet" title="Hebrew alphabet">Hebrew</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syriac_alphabet" title="Syriac alphabet">Syriac</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arabic_alphabet" title="Arabic alphabet">Arabic alphabets</a>. During its approximately 3,100 years of written history,<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Aramaic has served variously as a language of administration of empires, as a language of divine worship and religious study, and as the spoken tongue of a number of <a href="/wiki/Semitic_people" title="Semitic people">Semitic peoples</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a>.
</p><p>Historically, Aramaic was the language of the <a href="/wiki/Arameans" title="Arameans">Arameans</a>, a Semitic-speaking people of the region between the northern <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> and the northern <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a> valley. By around 1000 BC, the Arameans had a string of kingdoms in what is now part of <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>. Aramaic rose to prominence under the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Neo-Assyrian Empire</a> (911–605 BC), under whose influence Aramaic became a prestige language, and its use spread throughout most of <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> and the Levant. At its height, variants of Aramaic were spoken all over in what is today <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palestine_(region)" title="Palestine (region)">Palestine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Arabia" title="Eastern Arabia">Eastern Arabia</a>, Northern Arabia, and to a lesser extent parts of southeast and south central <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, and parts of northwest <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Aramaic was the <a href="/wiki/Language_of_Jesus" title="Language of Jesus">language of Jesus</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> who spoke the <a href="/wiki/Galilean_dialect" title="Galilean dialect">Galilean dialect</a> during his public ministry, as well as the language of large sections of the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">biblical</a> books of <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Daniel" title="Book of Daniel">Daniel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Ezra" title="Book of Ezra">Ezra</a>, and also one of the languages of the <a href="/wiki/Talmud" title="Talmud">Talmud</a>.
</p><p>The scribes of the Neo-Assyrian bureaucracy had also used Aramaic, and this practice—together with other administrative practices—was subsequently inherited by the succeeding <a href="/wiki/Neo-Babylonian_Empire" title="Neo-Babylonian Empire">Neo-Babylonians</a> (605–539 BC), and the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenids</a> (539–323 BC). Mediated by scribes that had been trained in the language, highly standardized <i>written</i> Aramaic (in its Achaemenid form called <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Aramaic" class="mw-redirect" title="Imperial Aramaic">Imperial Aramaic</a>) progressively also become the <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> of trade and commerce throughout the Achaemenid territories, which extended as far east as the Indus valley. (That use of <i>written</i> Aramaic subsequently led to the adoption of the <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_alphabet" title="Aramaic alphabet">Aramaic alphabet</a> and—as <a href="/wiki/Logogram" title="Logogram">logograms</a>—some Aramaic vocabulary in the <a href="/wiki/Pahlavi_scripts" title="Pahlavi scripts">Pahlavi scripts</a>, which were used by several <a href="/wiki/Middle_Iranian_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Middle Iranian languages">Middle Iranian languages</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Parthian_language" title="Parthian language">Parthian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Middle_Persian" title="Middle Persian">Middle Persian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sogdian_language" title="Sogdian language">Sogdian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Khwarazmian_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Khwarazmian language">Khwarazmian</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Green_1992_45_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Green_1992_45-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Aramaic's long history and diverse and widespread use has led to the development of many divergent varieties, which are sometimes considered <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">dialects</a>, though they have become distinct enough over time that they are now sometimes considered as separate <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">languages</a>. Therefore, there is not one singular, static Aramaic language; each time and place rather has had its own variation. The more widely spoken <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Aramaic" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Aramaic">Eastern Aramaic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mandaic_language" title="Mandaic language">Mandaic</a> forms are today largely restricted to <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Kurdistan" title="Iraqi Kurdistan">Iraqi Kurdistan</a>, northeastern Syria, northwestern Iran and southeastern Turkey, whilst the severely endangered <a href="/wiki/Western_Neo-Aramaic" title="Western Neo-Aramaic">Western Neo-Aramaic</a> is spoken by small communities in northwestern Syria.
</p><p>Certain dialects of Aramaic are also retained as a <a href="/wiki/Sacred_language" title="Sacred language">sacred language</a> by certain religious communities. One of those liturgical dialects is <a href="/wiki/Mandaic_language" title="Mandaic language">Mandaic</a>, which besides being a living variant of Aramaic is also the liturgical language of <a href="/wiki/Mandaeism" title="Mandaeism">Mandaeism</a>. Significantly more widespread is <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac</a>, the liturgical language of <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Christianity" title="Syriac Christianity">Syriac Christianity</a>, in particular the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Church_of_the_East" title="Assyrian Church of the East">Assyrian Church of the East</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Chaldean_Catholic_Church" title="Chaldean Catholic Church">Chaldean Catholic Church</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox_Church" title="Syriac Orthodox Church">Syriac Orthodox Church</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Pentecostal_Church" title="Assyrian Pentecostal Church">Assyrian Pentecostal Church</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Evangelical_Church" title="Assyrian Evangelical Church">Assyrian Evangelical Church</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Church_of_the_East" title="Ancient Church of the East">Ancient Church of the East</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Catholic_Church" title="Syriac Catholic Church">Syriac Catholic Church</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Maronite_Church" title="Maronite Church">Maronite Church</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Saint_Thomas_Christian_denominations" title="Saint Thomas Christian denominations">Saint Thomas Christian denominations</a> of <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Syriac was also the liturgical language of several now-extinct <a href="/wiki/Gnosticism" title="Gnosticism">gnostic</a> faiths, such as <a href="/wiki/Manichaeism" title="Manichaeism">Manichaeism</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Neo-Aramaic_languages" title="Neo-Aramaic languages">Neo-Aramaic languages</a> are still spoken today as a <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">first language</a> by many communities of Syriac Christians, <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a> (in particular, the <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_Jews" class="mw-redirect" title="Kurdish Jews">Kurdish Jews</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Mandaeans" title="Mandaeans">Mandaeans</a> of <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> most numerously by Chaldeans, Syriacs and Assyrians with numbers of fluent speakers ranging approximately from 1 million to 2 million, with the main languages among Assyrians being <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Neo-Aramaic" title="Assyrian Neo-Aramaic">Assyrian Neo-Aramaic</a> (235,000 speakers), <a href="/wiki/Chaldean_Neo-Aramaic" title="Chaldean Neo-Aramaic">Chaldean Neo-Aramaic</a> (1 million speakers) and <a href="/wiki/Turoyo_language" title="Turoyo language">Turoyo</a> (112,000 to 450,000 speakers), together with a number of smaller closely related languages with no more than 5,000 to 10,000 speakers between them. They have retained use of the once dominant lingua franca despite subsequent <a href="/wiki/Language_shift" title="Language shift">language shifts</a> experienced throughout the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>. However, the Aramaic languages are now considered <a href="/wiki/Endangered_language" title="Endangered language">endangered</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The languages are used by the older generation, all beyond retirement age, and so could go extinct within a generation. However, researchers are working to record all the dialects of Neo-Aramaic languages before they go extinct.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Royal Aramaic inscriptions from the Aramean city-states date from <a href="/wiki/10th_century_BC" title="10th century BC">10th century BC</a>, making Aramaic one of the world's <a href="/wiki/List_of_languages_by_first_written_accounts" title="List of languages by first written accounts">oldest recorded</a> <a href="/wiki/Modern_language" title="Modern language">living languages</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Richardp69_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richardp69-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aramaic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>"Aram" is used as a proper name of several people in the <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Torah</a> (Hebrew Bible) including descendants of Shem (<a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Genesis#10:22" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Genesis">Genesis 10:22</a>), Nahor (<a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Genesis#22:21" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Genesis">Genesis 22:21</a>), and Jacob (<a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/1_Chronicles#7:34" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/1 Chronicles">1 Chronicles 7:34</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Aram_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aram-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The ancient Greeks did not use the term Aramaic, instead referring to “Syrians”.<sup id="cite_ref-TN_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TN-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The connection between the names Syrian and Aramaic was made in 1835 by <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne_Marc_Quatrem%C3%A8re" title="Étienne Marc Quatremère">Étienne Marc Quatremère</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-TN_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TN-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
Ancient <a href="/wiki/Aram_(region)" title="Aram (region)">Aram</a>, bordering northern Israel and now called Syria, is considered the linguistic center of Aramaic, the language of the Arameans who settled the area during the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> circa 3500 BC. The language is often mistakenly considered to have originated within Assyria (Iraq). In fact, Arameans carried their language and writing into Mesopotamia by voluntary migration, by forced exile of conquering armies, and by nomadic <a href="/wiki/Chaldea" title="Chaldea">Chaldean</a> invasions of Babylonia during the period from 1200 to 1000 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Christian <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> uses the <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Koine Greek</a> word <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">Ἑβραϊστί</span> <i>Hebraïstí</i> to denote "Aramaic", as Aramaic was at that time the language commonly spoken by the <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Aram_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aram-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Egypt#Ptolemaic_and_Roman" title="History of the Jews in Egypt">Hellenized Jewish community of Alexandria</a> instead translated "Aramaic" to "the Syrian tongue".
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_distribution">Geographic distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aramaic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Geographic distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Syriac_inscription_at_Syro-Malabar_Catholic_Major_Archbishop%27s_House_Ernakulam.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Syriac_inscription_at_Syro-Malabar_Catholic_Major_Archbishop%27s_House_Ernakulam.jpg/220px-Syriac_inscription_at_Syro-Malabar_Catholic_Major_Archbishop%27s_House_Ernakulam.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Syriac_inscription_at_Syro-Malabar_Catholic_Major_Archbishop%27s_House_Ernakulam.jpg/330px-Syriac_inscription_at_Syro-Malabar_Catholic_Major_Archbishop%27s_House_Ernakulam.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Syriac_inscription_at_Syro-Malabar_Catholic_Major_Archbishop%27s_House_Ernakulam.jpg/440px-Syriac_inscription_at_Syro-Malabar_Catholic_Major_Archbishop%27s_House_Ernakulam.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1778" data-file-height="1300" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Syriac_inscription_at_Syro-Malabar_Catholic_Major_Archbishop%27s_House_Ernakulam.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac</a> inscription at the <a href="/wiki/Syro-Malabar_Catholic_Church" title="Syro-Malabar Catholic Church">Syro-Malabar Catholic Church</a>'s Major Archbishop's House in <a href="/wiki/Kerala" title="Kerala">Kerala</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_India" title="South India">South India</a></div></div></div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Neo-Assyrian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neo-Babylonian_Empire" title="Neo-Babylonian Empire">Neo-Babylonian Empires</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arameans" title="Arameans">Arameans</a>, the native speakers of Aramaic, began to settle in greater numbers, at first in <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a>, and later in <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyria</a> (<a href="/wiki/Upper_Mesopotamia" title="Upper Mesopotamia">Upper Mesopotamia</a>, modern-day northern <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, northeast <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, northwest <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, and south eastern <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> (what was Armenia at the time).<sup id="cite_ref-Richardp69_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richardp69-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The influx eventually resulted in the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Neo-Assyrian Empire</a> (911–605 BC) adopting an <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian</a>-influenced <a href="/wiki/Old_Aramaic_language#Imperial_Aramaic" title="Old Aramaic language">Imperial Aramaic</a> as the <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> of its empire.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> This policy was continued by the short-lived <a href="/wiki/Neo-Babylonian_Empire" title="Neo-Babylonian Empire">Neo-Babylonian Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes</a>, and all three empires became operationally bilingual in written sources, with Aramaic used alongside Akkadian. The <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a> (539–323 BC) continued this tradition, and the extensive influence of these empires led to Aramaic gradually becoming the <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a> of most of western Asia, the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Richardp69_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richardp69-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Beginning with the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun Caliphate</a> in the late 7th century, Arabic gradually replaced Aramaic as the lingua franca of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>. However, Aramaic remains a spoken, literary, and liturgical language for local Christians and also some <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a>. Aramaic also continues to be spoken by the Assyrians of Iraq, northeastern Syria, southeastern Turkey and northwest Iran, with diaspora communities in <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a> and southern <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Mandaeans" title="Mandaeans">Mandaeans</a> also continue to use <a href="/wiki/Mandaic_language" title="Mandaic language">Mandaic Aramaic</a> as a liturgical language, although most now speak Arabic as their <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">first language</a>. There are still also a small number of first-language speakers of <a href="/wiki/Western_Aramaic_languages" title="Western Aramaic languages">Western Aramaic varieties</a> in isolated villages in western Syria.
</p><p>The turbulence of the last two centuries (particularly the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_genocide" title="Assyrian genocide">Assyrian genocide</a>) has seen speakers of first-language and literary Aramaic dispersed throughout the world. However, there are a number of sizable Assyrian towns in northern Iraq such as <a href="/wiki/Alqosh" title="Alqosh">Alqosh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bakhdida" title="Bakhdida">Bakhdida</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bartella" title="Bartella">Bartella</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tesqopa" title="Tesqopa">Tesqopa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tel_Keppe" title="Tel Keppe">Tel Keppe</a>, and numerous small villages, where Aramaic is still the main spoken language, and many large cities in this region also have Assyrian Aramaic-speaking communities, particularly <a href="/wiki/Mosul" title="Mosul">Mosul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erbil" title="Erbil">Erbil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kirkuk" title="Kirkuk">Kirkuk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dohuk" class="mw-redirect" title="Dohuk">Dohuk</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Al-Hasakah" title="Al-Hasakah">al-Hasakah</a>. In Modern Israel, the only native Aramaic speaking population are the <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_Jews" class="mw-redirect" title="Kurdish Jews">Kurdish Jews</a>, although the language is dying out.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> However, Aramaic is also experiencing a revival among <a href="/wiki/Maronites_in_Israel" title="Maronites in Israel">Maronites in Israel</a> in <a href="/wiki/Jish" title="Jish">Jish</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aramaic_languages_and_dialects">Aramaic languages and dialects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aramaic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Aramaic languages and dialects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:YeshuaDSS.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/YeshuaDSS.gif/220px-YeshuaDSS.gif" decoding="async" width="220" height="81" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8e/YeshuaDSS.gif 1.5x" data-file-width="310" data-file-height="114" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:YeshuaDSS.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"<a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a>" in Jewish Aramaic</div></div></div>
<p>Aramaic is often spoken of as a single language, but is in reality a group of related languages.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Some Aramaic languages differ more from each other than the <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance languages</a> do among themselves. Its long history, extensive literature, and use by different religious communities are all factors in the diversification of the language. Some Aramaic dialects are mutually intelligible, whereas others are not, not unlike the situation with modern <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Arabic" title="Varieties of Arabic">varieties of Arabic</a>. Some Aramaic languages are known under different names; for example, <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac</a> is particularly used to describe the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Aramaic_languages" title="Eastern Aramaic languages">Eastern Aramaic variety</a> used in Christian ethnic communities in Iraq, southeastern Turkey, northeastern Syria, and northwestern Iran, and <a href="/wiki/Saint_Thomas_Christians" title="Saint Thomas Christians">Saint Thomas Christians</a> in India. Most dialects can be described as either "Eastern" or "Western", the dividing line being roughly the <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a>, or slightly west of it. It is also helpful to draw a distinction between those Aramaic languages that are modern living languages (often called "Neo-Aramaic"), those that are still in use as literary languages, and those that are extinct and are only of interest to scholars. Although there are some exceptions to this rule, this classification gives "Modern", "Middle", and "Old" periods, alongside "Eastern" and "Western" areas, to distinguish between the various languages and dialects that are Aramaic.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing_system">Writing system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aramaic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Writing system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_alphabet" title="Aramaic alphabet">Aramaic alphabet</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Amen_in_East_Syriac_Aramaic_language.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Amen_in_East_Syriac_Aramaic_language.svg/150px-Amen_in_East_Syriac_Aramaic_language.svg.png" decoding="async" width="150" height="125" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Amen_in_East_Syriac_Aramaic_language.svg/225px-Amen_in_East_Syriac_Aramaic_language.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Amen_in_East_Syriac_Aramaic_language.svg/300px-Amen_in_East_Syriac_Aramaic_language.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="120" data-file-height="100" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Amen_in_East_Syriac_Aramaic_language.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Amen in <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">East Syriac Aramaic</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Syriac_Sert%C3%A2_book_script.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Syriac_Sert%C3%A2_book_script.jpg/220px-Syriac_Sert%C3%A2_book_script.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Syriac_Sert%C3%A2_book_script.jpg/330px-Syriac_Sert%C3%A2_book_script.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Syriac_Sert%C3%A2_book_script.jpg/440px-Syriac_Sert%C3%A2_book_script.jpg 2x" data-file-width="991" data-file-height="748" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Syriac_Sert%C3%A2_book_script.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>11th century book in <a href="/wiki/Syriac_alphabet" title="Syriac alphabet">Syriac Serto</a></div></div></div>
<p>The earliest Aramaic alphabet was based on the <a href="/wiki/Phoenician_alphabet" title="Phoenician alphabet">Phoenician alphabet</a>. In time, Aramaic developed its distinctive "square" style. The ancient Israelites and other peoples of <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">Canaan</a> adopted this alphabet for writing their own languages. Thus, it is better known as the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_alphabet" title="Hebrew alphabet">Hebrew alphabet</a> today. This is the writing system used in <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Aramaic" title="Biblical Aramaic">Biblical Aramaic</a> and other Jewish writing in Aramaic. The other main writing system used for Aramaic was developed by Christian communities: a cursive form known as the <a href="/wiki/Syriac_alphabet" title="Syriac alphabet">Syriac alphabet</a>. A highly modified form of the Aramaic alphabet, the <a href="/wiki/Mandaic_alphabet" class="mw-redirect" title="Mandaic alphabet">Mandaic alphabet</a>, is used by the <a href="/wiki/Mandaeism" title="Mandaeism">Mandaeans</a>.
</p><p>In addition to these writing systems, certain derivatives of the Aramaic alphabet were used in ancient times by particular groups: the <a href="/wiki/Nabataean_alphabet" title="Nabataean alphabet">Nabataean alphabet</a> in <a href="/wiki/Petra" title="Petra">Petra</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Palmyrene_alphabet" title="Palmyrene alphabet">Palmyrene alphabet</a> in <a href="/wiki/Palmyra" title="Palmyra">Palmyra</a>. In modern times, <a href="/wiki/Turoyo_language" title="Turoyo language">Turoyo</a> (see <a href="#Modern_Eastern_Aramaic">below</a>) has sometimes been written in a <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin script</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aramaic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table border="0" cellpadding="5" style="float:right; background:#ffffd9; margin-left:10px;">

<tbody><tr>
<td style="background:#ffffd9;"><div class="timeline-wrapper"><map name="timeline_3a42660ca6acae46c3e45a32fa1bfab3"></map><img usemap="#timeline_3a42660ca6acae46c3e45a32fa1bfab3" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/timeline/3a42660ca6acae46c3e45a32fa1bfab3.png" /></div>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The history of Aramaic is broken down into three broad periods:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="#Old_Aramaic">Old Aramaic</a> (1100 BC–200 AD), including:
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Aramaic" title="Biblical Aramaic">Biblical Aramaic</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a>.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Language_of_Jesus" title="Language of Jesus">Aramaic of Jesus</a> (<a href="/wiki/Jewish_Palestinian_Aramaic" title="Jewish Palestinian Aramaic">Jewish Palestinian Aramaic</a>).</li></ul></li>
<li><a href="#Middle_Aramaic">Middle Aramaic</a> (200–1200), including:
<ul><li>Literary <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac</a>.</li>
<li>The Aramaic of the <a href="/wiki/Talmud" title="Talmud">Talmudim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Targum" title="Targum">Targumim</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Midrash" title="Midrash">Midrashim</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mandaic_language" title="Mandaic language">Mandaic</a>.</li></ul></li>
<li><a href="#Modern_Aramaic">Modern Aramaic</a> (1200–present), including:
<ul><li>Various modern vernaculars.</li></ul></li></ul>
<p>This classification is based on that used by Klaus Beyer.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_Aramaic">Old Aramaic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aramaic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Old Aramaic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Old_Aramaic_language" title="Old Aramaic language">Old Aramaic language</a></div>
<p>The term "Old Aramaic" is used to describe the varieties of the language from its first known use until the point roughly marked by the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian Empire</a> (224 AD), dominating the influential, eastern dialect region. As such, the term covers over thirteen centuries of the development of Aramaic. This vast time span includes all Aramaic that is now effectively extinct.
</p><p>The central phase in the development of Old Aramaic was its official use by the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a> (500–330 BC). The period before this, dubbed "Ancient Aramaic", saw the development of the language from being spoken in Aramaean city-states to become a major means of communication in diplomacy and trade throughout <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>. After the fall of the Achaemenid Empire, local vernaculars became increasingly prominent, fanning the divergence of an Aramaic <a href="/wiki/Dialect_continuum" title="Dialect continuum">dialect continuum</a> and the development of differing written standards.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Aramaic">Ancient Aramaic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aramaic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Ancient Aramaic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>"Ancient Aramaic" refers to the earliest known period of the language, from its origin until it becomes the lingua franca of the <a href="/wiki/Fertile_Crescent" title="Fertile Crescent">Fertile Crescent</a>. It was the language of the Aramean city-states of <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hama" title="Hama">Hamath</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arpad,_Syria" title="Arpad, Syria">Arpad</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bar-rakib.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Bar-rakib.jpg/220px-Bar-rakib.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Bar-rakib.jpg/330px-Bar-rakib.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/26/Bar-rakib.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="332" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bar-rakib.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ingot" title="Ingot">Silver ingot</a> of Bar-Rakib, son of Panammuwa II,<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> King of <a href="/wiki/Sam%27al" title="Sam&#39;al">Sam'al</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>There are inscriptions that evidence the earliest use of the language, dating from the 10th century BC. These inscriptions are mostly diplomatic documents between Aramaean city-states. The alphabet of Aramaic at this early period seems to be based on the <a href="/wiki/Phoenician_alphabet" title="Phoenician alphabet">Phoenician alphabet</a>, and there is a unity in the written language. It seems that, in time, a more refined alphabet, suited to the needs of the language, began to develop from this in the eastern regions of Aram. Due to increasing Aramean migration eastward, the Western periphery of Assyria became bilingual in Akkadian and Aramean at least as early as the mid-9th century BC. As the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Neo-Assyrian Empire</a> conquered Aramean lands west of the <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tiglath-Pileser_III" title="Tiglath-Pileser III">Tiglath-Pileser III</a> made Aramaic the Empire's second official language, and it eventually supplanted Akkadian completely.
</p><p>From 700 BC, the language began to spread in all directions, but lost much of its unity. Different dialects emerged in Assyria, Babylonia, the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>. Around 600 BC, Adon, a <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">Canaanite</a> king, used Aramaic to write to an Egyptian <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">Pharaoh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>"Chaldee" or "Chaldean Aramaic" used to be common terms for the Aramaic of the Chaldean dynasty of <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a>. It was used to describe <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Aramaic" title="Biblical Aramaic">Biblical Aramaic</a>, which was, however, written in a later style. It is not to be confused with the modern language <a href="/wiki/Chaldean_Neo-Aramaic" title="Chaldean Neo-Aramaic">Chaldean Neo-Aramaic</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Imperial_Aramaic">Imperial Aramaic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aramaic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Imperial Aramaic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks" style="float:right;clear:right;width:190px;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%;background:white; font-size:85%"><tbody><tr><th style="padding:0.2em 0.4em 0.2em;font-size:145%;line-height:1.2em;background:#ccf"><a href="/wiki/Arameans" title="Arameans">Arameans</a></th></tr><tr><td class="plainlist" style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Aramaic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Aramaic language">Aramaic language</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Aramaic_alphabet" title="Aramaic alphabet">Aramaic alphabet</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><th style="padding:0.1em;border-top:1px solid #ccc">

<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Irhuleni" title="Irhuleni">Irhuleni</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hezion" title="Hezion">Hezion</a></li>
